OIS Cafe — Operational Plan

Complete Operational Intelligence System for UK Cafes & Coffee Shops
Generated: 2026-03-08 Version: v1.0 — Gold Enrichment Architecture: 2-in-1 (Standalone + Chain)
The Real Story

This system maps the entire operational surface of a UK cafe — from the moment the espresso machine is switched on at dawn to the final cash reconciliation at close. It covers the 12 domains that determine whether a cafe thrives or merely survives: food safety compliance that keeps the EHO rating at 5, coffee craft that justifies a premium price, team culture that stops the revolving door of barista turnover, and the financial discipline that turns a passion project into a sustainable business. Every block is written for the reality of UK cafe operations — SFBB diaries, Natasha's Law labelling, ACAS-compliant employment practices, and the relentless economics of a 25% food cost target. The system serves both the independent owner pulling their own shots and the multi-site operator benchmarking across locations. It is not a manual — it is an investigative framework that diagnoses what's actually happening and prescribes action at three layers: immediate fixes, system building, and culture embedding.

Three-Layer Execution Model

LayerTimeframeFocusDescription
L1 0–2 weeks Immediate Actions Stop the bleeding. Daily habits, quick wins, critical compliance gaps. Every block's L1 can be started on Day 1 with no budget.
L2 2–8 weeks System Building Build the infrastructure. SOPs, training programmes, tracking systems, escalation pathways. This is where the cafe moves from reactive to proactive.
L3 8+ weeks Culture & Excellence Embed permanently. Dashboards, peer observation, governance meetings, annual reviews. The system runs itself.

Venue Types Supported

Independent Cafe
The single-site owner-operator. The heart of the UK high street.
Specialty Coffee
Third-wave, single-origin focused. Craft is the brand.
Chain Cafe
Multi-site company-owned. Consistency at scale.
Franchise Cafe
Franchised brand. Compliance + local autonomy.
Drive-Through
Speed-first format. Queue management is everything.
Kiosk
Compact footprint. Stripped-back efficiency.
Coffee Van
Mobile operation. Events, markets, offices.
Bakery Cafe
Food-forward. Pastries, bread, kitchen complexity.

D01

Food Safety & Hygiene

20 blocks · 98 tools
B001 Daily Temperature Monitoring 5 tools ENRICHED
Ensures all refrigeration and freezer units are checked at least twice daily, with temperatures logged and any excursions flagged immediately. This block forms the backbone of cold chain compliance and is the single most inspected element during EHO visits.
M01: Food Safety & Hygiene Compliance food_safetytemperatureSFBBEHO
View full detail
Why This Matters

Temperature monitoring is the single most critical daily food safety task in any cafe. Under EU-retained Regulation (EC) 852/2004 and the Food Safety Act 1990, food businesses must demonstrate that potentially hazardous foods are stored at safe temperatures — below 8°C for chilled goods (with best practice at 5°C or below) and below -18°C for frozen items. The Food Standards Agency's Safer Food Better Business (SFBB) pack specifically requires documented temperature checks as part of the 4 Cs (Cleaning, Cooking, Chilling, Cross-contamination). When an Environmental Health Officer (EHO) visits,...

L1 — Immediate

IMMEDIATE (0-2 weeks). Week 1: Purchase a calibrated digital probe thermometer (budget £15-30) and print a simple temperature log sheet with columns for date, time, unit name, temperature reading, initials, and corrective action. Identify every refrigeration unit in the cafe — typically the main under-counter fridge, display fridge, milk fridge behind the bar, and any chest or upright freezer. Lab...

L2 — System Build

SYSTEM BUILD (2-8 weeks). Move from paper logs to a structured tracking system. Create a Temperature Monitoring SOP document that covers: which units to check, acceptable ranges, probe calibration procedure (ice-water test monthly), escalation steps, and who is responsible on each shift. Train all staff to Level 2 Food Hygiene standard if not already certified — this is a legal requirement for foo...

L3 — Culture

CULTURE & EXCELLENCE (8+ weeks). Temperature monitoring should now be an automatic habit, not a chore. Introduce a visual dashboard near the staff area showing the current week's compliance rate (target: 100% of checks completed on time). Celebrate streaks — 30 consecutive days with no missed checks earns team recognition. Conduct quarterly internal audits where the manager or owner spot-checks lo...

Tools
  • T001-01 Daily Temperature Log Sheet (checklist — L1)
  • T001-02 Probe Calibration Record (tracker — L2)
  • T001-03 Temperature Excursion Corrective Action Log (template — L2)
  • T001-04 Monthly Temperature Compliance Summary (dashboard — L3)
  • T001-05 Temperature Monitoring SOP Template (SOP — L2)
KPIs
  • 100% of scheduled temperature checks completed on time (zero missed checks per week)
  • Zero temperature excursions lasting more than 2 hours without corrective action
  • Probe calibration completed monthly with variance within ±1°C
  • Food Hygiene Rating maintained at 5 (Very Good) at every EHO inspection

B002 Cold Chain Management SOP 5 tools ENRICHED
Establishes end-to-end cold chain procedures from delivery acceptance through to storage and cold-hold service. This block ensures that chilled and frozen products never enter the temperature danger zone (8-63°C) at any point within the cafe's control.
M01: Food Safety & Hygiene Compliance food_safetycold_chaindeliverystorage
View full detail
Why This Matters

The cold chain is the unbroken series of refrigerated steps that keeps perishable food safe from the moment it leaves the supplier to the moment it reaches the customer. In a cafe environment, the chain typically involves delivery van to backdoor, backdoor to fridge, fridge to preparation area, and potentially preparation area to display counter. Every break in this chain creates a window for bacterial growth. Under the Food Safety Act 1990 and EU-retained Regulation (EC) 852/2004, food businesses bear legal responsibility for maintaining safe temperatures from the point of delivery onwards. T...

L1 — Immediate

IMMEDIATE (0-2 weeks). Week 1: Map the cold chain pathway in your cafe. Trace the journey of a carton of milk from the delivery driver's hands to the customer's latte. Identify every point where temperature control could be lost: the back door area, the walk from door to fridge, the barista station, the display counter. On Day 1, create a simple Delivery Acceptance Checklist: when a delivery arriv...

L2 — System Build

SYSTEM BUILD (2-8 weeks). Draft a comprehensive Cold Chain Management SOP covering: delivery acceptance procedures, fridge organisation standards, stock rotation (FIFO), cold-hold protocols for display items, and the maximum time food can be out of refrigeration. Include photographs of correctly organised fridges as visual references. Create a Supplier Temperature Incident Log — every time a deliv...

L3 — Culture

CULTURE & EXCELLENCE (8+ weeks). Cold chain management should be second nature. Introduce quarterly supplier reviews where you share delivery temperature data with each supplier and discuss any concerns. Implement a cold chain champion role that rotates monthly among staff — this person has extra responsibility for checking deliveries and fridge organisation that week. Run biannual cold chain scen...

Tools
  • T002-01 Delivery Acceptance Checklist (checklist — L1)
  • T002-02 Cold Chain Management SOP (SOP — L2)
  • T002-03 Supplier Temperature Incident Log (tracker — L2)
  • T002-04 Weekly Fridge Organisation Audit (audit — L2)
  • T002-05 Cold Chain Compliance Dashboard (dashboard — L3)
KPIs
  • 100% of deliveries checked and logged with temperature readings on arrival
  • Zero chilled deliveries accepted above 8°C without documented corrective action
  • Weekly fridge audit score of 90% or above consistently
  • All opened/decanted items labelled with date and use-by at 100% compliance

B003 Temperature Excursion Response 4 tools ENRICHED
Provides a clear, step-by-step response protocol for when refrigeration temperatures breach safe limits. This block ensures that every staff member knows exactly what to do when a fridge or freezer reads too high, preventing unsafe food from reaching customers and creating the documented evidence tr...
M01: Food Safety & Hygiene Compliance food_safetytemperatureexcursioncorrective_action
View full detail
Why This Matters

A temperature excursion — when a fridge rises above 8°C or a freezer above -15°C — is one of the most common and dangerous food safety events in a cafe. The danger zone between 8°C and 63°C is where pathogenic bacteria multiply most rapidly, and some can double in number every 20 minutes. Under EU-retained Regulation (EC) 852/2004, food business operators must take immediate corrective action when cold chain failures occur, and under the Food Safety Act 1990, they must be able to demonstrate that they acted with due diligence. The SFBB pack includes a specific section on what to do when things...

L1 — Immediate

IMMEDIATE (0-2 weeks). Week 1: Create a Temperature Excursion Response Card — a laminated A5 card posted on or near every fridge and freezer. The card should contain: Step 1: Check the reading. Is the fridge above 8°C or the freezer above -15°C? If yes, proceed. Step 2: Do not open the unit unnecessarily. Each door opening raises the temperature further. Step 3: Check if the unit is actually fault...

L2 — System Build

SYSTEM BUILD (2-8 weeks). Develop a comprehensive Temperature Excursion SOP that expands on the response card with detailed guidance. Include a decision tree flowchart: discovery of high reading leads to initial checks, which leads to either simple cause identified (door left open — close and monitor) or equipment failure suspected (escalate). For equipment failure, the SOP should cover: contactin...

L3 — Culture

CULTURE & EXCELLENCE (8+ weeks). Temperature excursion response should be automatic and confident. Conduct quarterly excursion drills — simulate a failure and time how quickly staff respond correctly. Review excursion data quarterly to identify trends and invest in preventive measures: if a unit is failing regularly, replace it before a critical failure occurs. Consider installing wireless tempera...

Tools
  • T003-01 Temperature Excursion Response Card (protocol — L1)
  • T003-02 Excursion Incident Report Form (template — L2)
  • T003-03 Time-Temperature Stock Assessment Matrix (matrix — L2)
  • T003-04 Excursion Trend Tracker (tracker — L3)
KPIs
  • 100% of temperature excursions documented with completed incident reports
  • Staff response time under 15 minutes from discovery to corrective action initiated
  • Fewer than 4 excursion events per year across all refrigeration units
  • Zero instances of food served after being in the danger zone for more than 4 hours

Why This Matters

Allergen management is a matter of life and death. In the UK, approximately 2 million people live with a diagnosed food allergy, and allergic reactions cause around 10 deaths per year. The 14 allergens that must be declared under UK food law are: celery, cereals containing gluten, crustaceans, eggs, fish, lupin, milk, molluscs, mustard, nuts, peanuts, sesame, soybeans, and sulphur dioxide. The Food Information Regulations 2014 (as amended) require food businesses to provide allergen information for all food sold or provided. For non-prepacked food (the majority of what cafes serve), allergen i...

For each item, list every single ingredient, including sub-ingredients (e.g., if you use a premade cake from a supplier, you need their full ingredient list). Against each ingredient, flag which of the 14 allergens are present. Thi...

Create digital versions of the allergen matrix that can be easily updated when recipes or suppliers change. Conduct quarterly allergen audits: pick 5 menu items at random, trace their ingredients, and verify the allergen matrix is correct. Introduce mystery shopper-style allergen queries where the manager or a trusted friend asks a staff member about allergens and assesses the ...

B006 PPDS Compliance System 5 tools ENRICHED
Ensures all Prepacked for Direct Sale (PPDS) items are correctly labelled with full ingredient lists and allergen declarations in compliance with Natasha's Law. This block protects customers with food allergies and shields the business from prosecution under food safety legislation.
M01: Food Safety & Hygiene Compliance food_safetyallergensPPDSNatashas_Law
View full detail
Why This Matters

Natasha's Law (the Food Information (Amendment) (England) Regulations 2019, effective 1 October 2021) was introduced following the death of Natasha Ednan-Laperouse, who suffered a fatal allergic reaction to a Pret A Manger baguette that lacked allergen labelling. The law requires any food that is prepacked for direct sale — meaning wrapped or packaged on the premises before a customer selects or orders it — to carry a label showing the food name, full ingredients list, and the 14 declarable allergens emphasised within that list. This applies to sandwiches wrapped in cling film, boxed salads, s...

L1 — Immediate

IMMEDIATE (0-2 weeks). Week 1: Audit every item currently sold in the cafe to determine which qualify as PPDS. The key test is simple — if the item is packaged before the customer chooses it and before they have the opportunity to ask about allergens, it is PPDS and requires a label. Common cafe PPDS items include pre-wrapped sandwiches, boxed salads, sealed cake slices, pre-bottled smoothies or j...

L2 — System Build

SYSTEM BUILD (2-8 weeks). Create a master ingredients database — a spreadsheet or simple database linking every recipe to its full ingredient breakdown and allergen declarations. When a supplier changes a product formulation (e.g., the bread supplier adds sesame to their seeded loaf), the database must be updated and all affected labels reprinted the same day. Establish a label change management p...

L3 — Culture

CULTURE & EXCELLENCE (8+ weeks). Integrate PPDS compliance into the daily opening routine so thoroughly that serving an unlabelled PPDS item becomes unthinkable. Conduct quarterly PPDS audits where the manager selects ten random items and checks labels against the master database, recording any discrepancies. Invest in label design improvements — consider branded labels that reinforce the cafe's i...

Tools
  • T006-01 PPDS Item Audit Checklist (checklist — L1)

  • T006-03 PPDS Label Template (template — L1)
  • T006-04 Label Production SOP (SOP — L2)
  • T006-05 Daily PPDS Label Verification Log (checklist — L2)
KPIs
  • 100% of PPDS items on display carry a compliant label at all times
  • Zero allergen labelling errors detected in quarterly audits
  • Master ingredients database updated within 24 hours of any supplier formulation change
  • All food preparation staff trained on PPDS requirements within first week of employment

B009 Handwashing Protocol 5 tools ENRICHED
Establishes a clear, enforced handwashing protocol specifying when, how, and for how long staff must wash their hands, with compliance monitoring to prevent pathogen transmission. This block is the foundation of personal hygiene in any food business and a key element inspected by EHOs.
M01: Food Safety & Hygiene Compliance food_safetyhygienehandwashingpersonal_hygiene
View full detail
Why This Matters

Hand contamination is the single most common route of pathogen transmission in food businesses. The Food Standards Agency's Safer Food Better Business (SFBB) pack identifies handwashing as a cornerstone of the '4 Cs' framework under Cross-contamination prevention. EU-retained Regulation (EC) 852/2004, Annex II, Chapter I requires food premises to provide an adequate number of washbasins for cleaning hands, supplied with hot and cold running water, soap, and hygienic drying facilities. These washbasins must be separate from those used for washing food. During an EHO inspection, the officer will...

L1 — Immediate

IMMEDIATE (0-2 weeks). Week 1: Inspect all handwashing facilities in the cafe. There must be at least one dedicated handwash basin that is not used for food preparation or washing equipment. The basin must have hot and cold (or warm) running water, antibacterial soap in a wall-mounted dispenser (not a shared bar of soap), and hygienic hand drying — either paper towels from a dispenser or a hot air...

L2 — System Build

SYSTEM BUILD (2-8 weeks). Develop a Handwashing SOP document that codifies the technique, trigger points, facility requirements, and monitoring procedures. Include photographs showing correct technique. Integrate handwashing compliance into the daily manager walk-around — during every shift, the manager should observe at least one handwashing event and check that facilities are stocked. Introduce ...

L3 — Culture

CULTURE & EXCELLENCE (8+ weeks). Handwashing should now be automatic behaviour — staff should feel uncomfortable if they have not washed their hands, like leaving the house without keys. Conduct quarterly handwashing audits where the manager observes technique and timing without announcing the audit in advance. Track compliance trends and share results with the team — aim for 100% observed complia...

Tools
  • T009-01 Handwashing Instruction Poster (guide — L1)
  • T009-02 Handwashing Compliance Log (checklist — L1)
  • T009-03 Handwashing Facility Check Log (checklist — L2)

  • T009-05 Quarterly Handwashing Audit Form (audit — L3)
KPIs
  • 100% of handwashing facilities stocked and functional at all times during service
  • Zero observed instances of staff handling food without proper handwashing
  • Quarterly audit scores averaging 95% or above for technique and trigger point compliance
  • All new starters complete handwashing demonstration before handling any food

B010 Cross-Contamination Prevention 5 tools ENRICHED
Prevents the transfer of harmful bacteria and allergens between foods, surfaces, and equipment through colour-coded chopping boards, dedicated utensils, and workflow separation. This block addresses one of the most common causes of foodborne illness and EHO non-compliance findings in UK cafes.
M01: Food Safety & Hygiene Compliance food_safetycross_contaminationkitchencolour_coding
View full detail
Why This Matters

Cross-contamination is one of the four pillars of the Food Standards Agency's Safer Food Better Business (SFBB) framework — the '4 Cs' of Cleaning, Cooking, Chilling, and Cross-contamination. It occurs when harmful bacteria or allergens are transferred from one food, surface, or piece of equipment to another. In a cafe kitchen, this can happen in seconds: a knife used to cut raw chicken is then used to slice bread; a prep surface is wiped with a cloth that was previously used on raw meat; or a barista touches a nut-topped cake and then handles a customer's sandwich. The consequences range from...

L1 — Immediate

IMMEDIATE (0-2 weeks). Week 1: Purchase and implement a colour-coded chopping board system following the UK industry standard: Red for raw meat, Blue for raw fish, Yellow for cooked meat, Green for salad and fruit, Brown for vegetables, White for bakery and dairy. Label each board clearly and store them in a visible rack so the correct board is always easy to grab. If the cafe does minimal raw foo...

L2 — System Build

SYSTEM BUILD (2-8 weeks). Develop a Cross-Contamination Prevention SOP covering: the colour-coding system, utensil assignment, workflow separation, fridge storage rules, cleaning procedures between tasks, and allergen cross-contact controls. Map the kitchen workflow to identify potential cross-contamination points — where do raw and ready-to-eat preparation zones overlap? Can the layout be improve...

L3 — Culture

CULTURE & EXCELLENCE (8+ weeks). Cross-contamination prevention should be automatic muscle memory for all kitchen staff. Conduct quarterly internal audits that include observing food preparation practices during a normal service period — not just checking paperwork but watching what actually happens. Use ATP (adenosine triphosphate) swab testing on surfaces to objectively measure cleaning effectiv...

  • T010-04 Kitchen Workflow Risk Map (template — L2)
  • T010-05 Quarterly Cross-Contamination Audit (audit — L3)

Why This Matters

The order-taking interaction is the single biggest controllable factor in queue speed. In a typical UK cafe, the time from a customer reaching the till to the order being entered and payment taken ranges from 30 seconds for a simple black coffee to 90 seconds or more for a complex order with modifications. During the morning rush, when the cafe might serve 60 customers per hour, even a 10-second improvement per order gains 10 minutes — enough to serve an additional 6-8 customers. The order-taking moment is also the customer's primary human interaction with the cafe. It sets the tone for their ...

L1 — Immediate

IMMEDIATE (0-2 weeks). Week 1: Time the order-taking process during one peak period. Record the time from when the customer reaches the till to when payment is completed for 20 orders. Calculate the average. Identify the top three time wasters — the moments where the process stalls or slows. Common culprits: customers studying the menu at the till (solution: better menu visibility from the queue),...

L2 — System Build

SYSTEM BUILD (2-8 weeks). Optimise the POS system for speed: learn every shortcut, create quick-access buttons for the top 10 drinks, set up modifier sequences that match the order-taking flow. If the POS does not support quick access, consider whether it is the right system for a busy cafe. Develop a menu knowledge training programme: every till operator must be able to answer any menu question i...

L3 — Culture

CULTURE & EXCELLENCE (8+ weeks). Continuously refine the order-taking process through observation and data. Track average order time monthly and set improvement targets. Observe the best performers and identify what they do differently — share their techniques with the team. Review the menu regularly for items that create order confusion or take too long to explain. Consider seasonal menu simplifi...

Why This Matters

Most cafe owners think about revenue in terms of daily takings and individual transactions. When considering a new staff position, calculate the CLV of the additional customers that person could serve during peak per...

Why This Matters

The customer who complains is giving the cafe a chance to fix the issue and retain their loyalty. The customers who leave silently are the real problem — they take their revenue to a competitor and often tell friends and social media about their bad experience without ever giving the cafe a chance to respond. Why This Matters

The grinder does more to determine espresso quality than the espresso machine itself. A £5,000 espresso machine paired with a neglected grinder will produce worse coffee than a £2,000 machine paired with a well-maintained one. Coffee grinders work by crushing beans between burrs — either flat or conical steel or ceramic discs — and these burrs wear down with use. A typical commercial cafe grinding 5-10kg of coffee per day will wear through a set of flat burrs in 6-12 months, though this varies significantly by grinder model and burr material. Why This Matters

Coffee is approximately 98% water. The quality of the water used to brew espresso has a profound impact on flavour, extraction, and the longevity of expensive equipment. In the UK, mains water quality varies enormously by region. London and the South East have notoriously hard water, with calcium carbonate levels frequently exceeding 300 parts per million (ppm). In contrast, areas of Scotland and Wales may have very soft water below 50 ppm. Neither extreme is ideal for coffee. Why This Matters

The menu is the single most influential piece of marketing material in a cafe. Every customer interacts with it, and its design directly affects what they order, how much they spend, and how they perceive value. Yet most cafe menus are designed by accident — items are added when someone has an idea, prices are set by copying competitors or adding a round-number markup, and the layout is determined by what fits on the board. Menu engineering is the discipline of replacing intuition with data. It combines sales mix data (what sells) with contribution margin data (what makes money) to categorise ...

L1 — Immediate

IMMEDIATE (0-2 weeks). Week 1: Gather the data needed for menu engineering. For each menu item (food and drink), you need two figures: the number sold per week (from your EPOS system or till records) and the contribution margin (selling price minus ingredient cost, using the costs calculated from B054 recipe cards and B055 portion control). If you do not have EPOS data, estimate weekly sales from ...

L2 — System Build

SYSTEM BUILD (2-8 weeks). Redesign the menu board or printed menu using menu engineering principles. Place Star and Puzzle items in the 'sweet spots' — the areas where customers' eyes naturally land first (top-right of a menu board, the first and last positions in a list, items with photos or borders that draw attention). Use pricing psychology: avoid currency symbols where possible (psychological...

L3 — Culture

CULTURE & EXCELLENCE (8+ weeks). Menu engineering should become a continuous optimisation discipline, not a one-off exercise. Run the full matrix analysis quarterly, comparing trends over time. Is the flat white becoming a Plowhorse as milk costs rise? Has the new seasonal cake become a Star? Use these trends to make proactive adjustments before margins erode. Experiment with pricing strategies: t...

  • Overall menu contribution margin exceeds target (typically 70-75% for drinks, 65-70% for food)

  • Star items maintain or grow their sales volume quarter on quarter
  • Menu size does not exceed optimal range (7-10 drink options, 8-12 food options)

Why This Matters

Without systematic quality evaluation, a cafe has no way of knowing whether its coffee is genuinely good or merely familiar. The human palate adapts quickly — baristas who taste the same espresso every day lose perspective on whether it has drifted. A coffee that was bright and sweet when the bag was opened can become flat and ashy by day 10 post-roast without anyone noticing, because the change happens gradually. Regular cupping — the industry-standard method of evaluating coffee by breaking a crust of grounds in hot water and slurping from a spoon — cuts through this habituation by forcing s...

Expand cupping beyond evaluation into discovery: taste coffees from origins or processes you have never served, evaluate experimental lots from roasters, and use cupping to make evidence-based purchasing decisions. Run team triangulation tests monthly — three cups, two identi...

Why This Matters

Why This Matters

The morning rush is the defining trading period for most UK cafes. In many venues, 40-60% of daily revenue is generated between 7:30am and 10:00am. How the team performs during these ninety minutes to two hours determines not just the day's financial outcome but the cafe's reputation. Customers during the morning rush are typically commuters and parents — time-poor, habitual, and unforgiving of delays. They want their coffee made correctly and quickly, every single day. Why This Matters

In a typical UK cafe, there are at least two distinct shift teams each day — an opening crew and an afternoon/closing crew — and often a third covering the lunch rush. Each shift change represents a potential rupture in operational continuity. Without a formal handover system, critical information evaporates: the morning team discovers a blocked drain but leaves before the plumber arrives, and the afternoon team has no idea the plumber is coming or what the issue was. A customer complains about their sandwich at 11am, the opening team promises a replacement on their next visit, but the afterno...

L1 — Immediate

Week 1: Create a simple A4 Shift Handover Log with sections for: Date and Shift (AM/PM), Outgoing Shift Lead name, Key Issues (anything the next team needs to know), Equipment Status (any faults or quirks), Stock Alerts (items running low or out), Customer Notes (complaints, promises, VIP visits), Outstanding Tasks (anything started but not finished), and Incoming Shift Lead...

L2 — System Build

Why This Matters

Food cost percentage is the pulse of a cafe's food profitability. If the cafe's food revenue is £2,000 per week and food purchases total £600, the food cost percentage is 30%. If that drifts to 38% — an increase that can happen gradually through supplier price increases, portion creep, and waste — the cafe loses £160 per week in GP, or £8,320 per year. That £8,320 is often the difference between a profitable cafe and a struggling one. The insidious nature of food cost drift is that it happens slowly: a supplier increases prices by 3% without notice, the team starts putting slightly more cheese...

B116 FIFO & Stock Rotation 4 tools ENRICHED
Implements First-In-First-Out (FIFO) stock rotation across all storage areas, ensuring the oldest stock is always used first to minimise waste from expiry, maintain freshness, and comply with food safety regulations.
M06: Supply Chain & Supplier Relations supply_chainFIFOstock_rotationfood_waste
View full detail
Why This Matters

FIFO is one of the simplest concepts in stock management and one of the most frequently violated. The principle is straightforward: the first items to arrive should be the first items used. New deliveries go behind or beneath existing stock so that older items are naturally pulled forward. When FIFO is not followed, the consequences are predictable and expensive: milk delivered on Monday gets pushed to the back of the fridge when Wednesday's delivery arrives, and by Thursday the Monday milk is approaching its use-by date — if not past it. Sandwich fillings opened on Tuesday are buried behind f...

L1 — Immediate

IMMEDIATE (0-2 weeks). Week 1: Implement a universal date labelling system. Purchase a roll of food-safe day-dot labels or use a label maker. From Day 1, every perishable item that enters the cafe must be labelled with either the delivery date (for items with printed use-by dates) or the date opened/prepared (for items decanted from bulk packaging). Establish a simple colour-coded day-dot system: ...

L2 — System Build

SYSTEM BUILD (2-8 weeks). Create a FIFO and Stock Rotation SOP covering: date labelling standards, rotation procedure for each storage area, daily check requirements, disposal procedure for expired items, and team responsibilities. Define use-by and best-before handling rules clearly for the team: use-by dates are legal deadlines and food must not be sold past them; best-before dates indicate qual...

L3 — Culture

CULTURE & EXCELLENCE (8+ weeks). FIFO should be invisible — so deeply embedded in the team's habits that it happens automatically without thought. Conduct quarterly FIFO audits: the manager checks all storage areas unannounced and records whether rotation is correct. Any area with old stock behind new stock triggers a team refresher. Set a target of zero expired items found in any storage area at ...

Tools
  • T116-01 Date Labelling Standards Card (checklist — L1)

  • T116-04 Quarterly FIFO Audit Template (template — L3)
KPIs
  • Zero expired items found in any storage area during routine or audit checks

  • Customer satisfaction scores maintained at or above pre-implementation levels (upselling should not reduce satisfaction)

Why This Matters

Procurement is typically the second-largest cost category for a cafe after labour, accounting for 25–40% of revenue. In a multi-site operation where each site orders independently, the business is paying the small-buyer price at every location, missing volume discounts that could save 5–15% across key categories. A chain buying 500kg of coffee per month across 8 sites should pay significantly less per kilogram than each site ordering 60kg independently. Why This Matters

Under the Health and Safety at Work etc. Act 1974, every employer has a legal duty to ensure, so far as is reasonably practicable, the health, safety, and welfare of all employees. The Management of Health and Safety at Work Regulations 1999 require every employer with five or more employees to conduct and record a suitable and sufficient risk assessment. Even cafes with fewer than five employees must conduct a risk assessment — they simply are not required to record it in writing, although best practice strongly recommends doing so. The Health and Safety Executive (HSE) can inspect any workpl...

Walk through every area of the cafe with a notebook: bar area, kitchen/prep, storage, customer seating, toilets, entrance/exit, and any outdoor areas. For each area, list every hazard you can identify — hot surfaces, sharp objects, wet floors, heavy ite...

L2 — System Build

SYSTEM BUILD (2–8 weeks). Develop a Health and Safety SOP library covering the top risks: burns and scalds prevention, manual handling technique, slip prevention and wet floor procedures, chemical handling (COSHH), and electrical safety. Introduce a near-miss reporting system — a simple form where staff can record incidents that nearly caused harm. Near-miss data is invaluable for identifying emer...

Establish a health and safety committee that meets quarterly to review risks, near-misses, and improvement ideas. Conduct annual external health and safety audits. Benchmark accident rates against industry data from HSE hospitality statistics. Invest in additional controls for persiste...

Why This Matters

The Regulatory Reform (Fire Safety) Order 2005 places full responsibility for fire safety on the responsible person — in most cafes, this is the owner or operator. Failure to comply is a criminal offence carrying penalties of unlimited fines and up to two years in prison. Following the Grenfell Tower tragedy and subsequent Fire Safety Act 2021, enforcement has tightened significantly. A cafe presents multiple fire risks: gas cookers and ovens, electrical equipment (grinders, dishwashers, EPOS systems), overloaded sockets, flammable cleaning chemicals, paper and cardboard storage, and extractio...

L1 — Immediate

IMMEDIATE (0–2 weeks). Week 1: Obtain a fire risk assessment template appropriate for small commercial premises or use T184-01. Walk through the premises identifying: all ignition sources (ovens, grills, toasters, electrical equipment, smoking areas), all fuel sources (paper, cardboard, cooking oils, cleaning chemicals, soft furnishings), and all people at risk. Check that fire alarm system or man...

L2 — System Build

SYSTEM BUILD (2–8 weeks). Complete the full fire risk assessment document and file it on the premises. Establish a fire safety log book recording: weekly fire alarm tests, monthly emergency lighting checks, annual fire extinguisher servicing, six-monthly fire drill records, and any fire safety issues identified and resolved. Train all staff on fire extinguisher use. Register for a free business fi...

L3 — Culture

CULTURE & EXCELLENCE (8+ weeks). Conduct fire drills at least every six months, varying the scenario. Debrief after each drill and document learnings. Install additional fire prevention measures where appropriate: automatic fire suppression in extraction hoods, heat detectors in kitchen areas, linked fire alarm systems. Review the fire risk assessment annually or whenever there is a significant ch...

Why This Matters

A cafe operates under a complex web of licensing and employment law that many owners do not fully understand until they face enforcement action. Every cafe must be registered as a food business with the local authority at least 28 days before opening — failure to register is a criminal offence. If the cafe plays any background music (including radio or streaming services), it needs both a PPL and PRS licence. Why This Matters

The UK cafe sector produces an estimated 2.5 billion disposable cups annually, with fewer than 1% recycled effectively. Beyond cups, cafes consume significant energy through espresso machines running 12+ hours daily, refrigeration units, dishwashers, and lighting. The average small cafe generates 20-40kg of waste daily, much of which is coffee grounds, food waste, and packaging that could be diverted from landfill. B059, B110

Chain & Multi-Site Architecture

The 2-in-1 design means standalone cafes and chains share the same diagnostic engine but with chain-specific signals (S197–S204), failure modes (FM102–FM108), and dedicated blocks (B187–B194) that only activate for chain_cafe and franchise_cafe venue types. Module M15 handles cross-site consistency, area management, franchise compliance, and central procurement.
